<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Marine mammal</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Humpback_Whale_underwater_shot.jpg"><img alt="A humpback whale swimming" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="1034" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Humpback_Whale_underwater_shot.jpg/300px-Humpback_Whale_underwater_shot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Humpback_Whale_underwater_shot.jpg/450px-Humpback_Whale_underwater_shot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Humpback_Whale_underwater_shot.jpg/600px-Humpback_Whale_underwater_shot.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Humpback_Whale_underwater_shot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Humpback_whale" title="Humpback whale">humpback whale</a> (<i>Megaptera novaeangliae</i>), a member of infraorder <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">Cetacea</a> of the order <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetartiodactyla" title="Cetartiodactyla">Cetartiodactyla</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hydrurga_leptonyx_edit1.jpg"><img alt="A black-speckled seal with a light-gray underside and a dark-gray back, sitting on rocks, its mouth agape showing sharp teeth" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1389" data-file-width="2000" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Hydrurga_leptonyx_edit1.jpg/300px-Hydrurga_leptonyx_edit1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Hydrurga_leptonyx_edit1.jpg/450px-Hydrurga_leptonyx_edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Hydrurga_leptonyx_edit1.jpg/600px-Hydrurga_leptonyx_edit1.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hydrurga_leptonyx_edit1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Leopard_seal" title="Leopard seal">leopard seal</a> (<i>Hydrurga leptonyx</i>), a member of the clade <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">Pinnipedia</a> of the order <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">Carnivora</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Marine mammals</b> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquatic_mammals" title="Aquatic mammals">aquatic mammals</a> that rely on the ocean and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystems" title="Marine ecosystems">marine ecosystems</a> for their existence. They include animals such as <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sirenia" title="Sirenia">manatees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_otter" title="Sea otter">sea otters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">polar bears</a>. They do not represent a distinct taxon or systematic grouping, but rather have a <a href="/wiki/Polyphyly" title="Polyphyly">polyphyletic</a> relation due to <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>, as in they do not have an immediate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_ancestor" title="Common ancestor">common ancestor</a>. They are also unified by their reliance on the marine environment for feeding.</p>
<p>Marine mammal adaptation to an aquatic lifestyle varies considerably between species. Both cetaceans and sirenians are fully aquatic and therefore are obligate water dwellers. Seals and sea-lions are semiaquatic; they spend the majority of their time in the water, but need to return to land for important activities such as <a href="/wiki/Mating" title="Mating">mating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breeding_in_the_wild" title="Breeding in the wild">breeding</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molting" title="Molting">molting</a>. In contrast, both otters and the polar bear are much less adapted to aquatic living. Their diet varies considerably as well; some may eat <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a>, others may eat fish, squid, shellfish, sea-grass and a few may eat other mammals. While the number of marine mammals is small compared to those found on land, their roles in various ecosystems are large, especially concerning the maintenance of marine ecosystems, through processes including the regulation of prey populations. This role in maintaining ecosystems makes them of particular concern as 23% of marine mammal species are currently threatened.</p>
<p>Marine mammals were first hunted by <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_whaling" title="Aboriginal whaling">aboriginal peoples</a> for food and other resources. Many were also the target for commercial industry, leading to a sharp decline in all populations of exploited species, such as whales and seals. Commercial hunting lead to the extinction of †<a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_sea_cow" title="Steller's sea cow">Steller's sea cow</a> and the †<a href="/wiki/Caribbean_monk_seal" title="Caribbean monk seal">Caribbean monk seal</a>. After commercial hunting ended, some species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Gray_whale" title="Gray whale">gray whale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_elephant_seal" title="Northern elephant seal">northern elephant seal</a>, have rebounded in numbers; conversely, other species, such as the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_right_whale" title="North Atlantic right whale">North Atlantic right whale</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Critically_endangered" title="Critically endangered">critically endangered</a>. Other than hunting, marine mammals can be killed as <a href="/wiki/Bycatch" title="Bycatch">bycatch</a> from fisheries, where they become entangled in fixed netting and drown or starve. Increased ocean traffic causes collisions between fast ocean vessels and large marine mammals. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_degradation" title="Habitat degradation">Habitat degradation</a> also threatens marine mammals and their ability to find and catch food. <a href="/wiki/Noise_pollution" title="Noise pollution">Noise pollution</a>, for example, may adversely affect <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocating</a> mammals, and the ongoing effects of global warming degrade arctic environments.</p>
<p></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marine_mammal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prorastomus_BW.jpg"><img alt="A furry, streamlined mammal swimming through the water with toes visible on each foot, similar to those of an elephant. All limbs are thrust backwards or underneath the animal." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="800" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Prorastomus_BW.jpg/220px-Prorastomus_BW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Prorastomus_BW.jpg/330px-Prorastomus_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Prorastomus_BW.jpg/440px-Prorastomus_BW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prorastomus_BW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustration of †<i><a href="/wiki/Prorastomus" title="Prorastomus">Prorastomus</a></i>, an early sirenian (40 mya)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Marine mammals form a diverse group of 129 species that rely on the ocean for their existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kasc_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-kasc-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pompa_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-pompa-4">[4]</a></sup> They do not represent a distinct taxon or systematic grouping, but instead have a <a href="/wiki/Polyphyly" title="Polyphyly">polyphyletic</a> relationship. They are also unified by their reliance on the marine environment for feeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jeff_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-jeff-5">[5]</a></sup> Despite the diversity in morphology seen between groups, improved foraging efficiency has been the main driver in their <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savage_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-savage-7">[7]</a></sup> The level of dependence on the marine environment for existence varies considerably with species. For example, dolphins and whales are completely dependent on the marine environment for all stages of their life; <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a> feed in the ocean but breed on land; and polar bears must feed on land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jeff_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-jeff-5">[5]</a></sup> Twenty three percent of marine mammal species are <a href="/wiki/Threatened_species" title="Threatened species">threatened</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ship_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ship-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cetaceans became aquatic around 50 million years ago (mya).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Based on molecular and morphological research, the cetaceans genetically and morphologically fall firmly within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artiodactyla" title="Artiodactyla">Artiodactyla</a> (even-toed ungulates).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The term "Cetartiodactyla" reflects the idea that whales evolved within the ungulates. The term was coined by merging the name for the two orders, Cetacea and Artiodactyla, into a single word. Under this definition, the closest living land relative of the whales and dolphins is thought to be the <a href="/wiki/Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus">hippopotamuses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sirenians, the sea cows, became aquatic around 40 million years ago. The first appearance of sirenians in the fossil record was during the early Eocene, and by the late Eocene, sirenians had significantly diversified. Inhabitants of rivers, estuaries, and nearshore marine waters, they were able to spread rapidly. The most primitive sirenian, †<i><a href="/wiki/Prorastomus" title="Prorastomus">Prorastomus</a></i>, was found in Jamaica,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savage_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-savage-7">[7]</a></sup> unlike other marine mammals which originated from the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> (such as cetaceans<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>). The first known quadrupedal sirenian was †<i><a href="/wiki/Pezosiren" title="Pezosiren">Pezosiren</a></i> from the early Eocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The earliest known sea cows, of the families †<a href="/wiki/Prorastomidae" title="Prorastomidae">Prorastomidae</a> and †<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protosirenidae" title="Protosirenidae">Protosirenidae</a>, were both confined to the Eocene, and were pig-sized, four-legged, amphibious creatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prins_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-prins-19">[19]</a></sup> The first members of <a href="/wiki/Dugongidae" title="Dugongidae">Dugongidae</a> appeared by the end of the Eocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> At this point, sea cows were fully aquatic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prins_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-prins-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pinnipeds <a href="/wiki/Cladogenesis" title="Cladogenesis">split</a> from other caniforms 50 mya during the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>. Their evolutionary link to terrestrial mammals was unknown until the 2007 discovery of †<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puijila_darwini" title="Puijila darwini">Puijila darwini</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Early_Miocene" title="Early Miocene">early Miocene</a> deposits in <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, Canada. Like a modern otter, †<i>Puijila</i> had a long tail, short limbs and webbed feet instead of flippers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawson2009_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawson2009-21">[21]</a></sup> The lineages of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otariidae" title="Otariidae">Otariidae</a> (eared seals) and <a href="/wiki/Odobenidae" title="Odobenidae">Odobenidae</a> (walrus) split almost 28 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnason2006_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arnason2006-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Earless_seal" title="Earless seal">Phocids</a> (earless seals) are known to have existed for at least 15 mya,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrin2009pp._861.E2.80.93866_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrin2009pp._861.E2.80.93866-23">[23]</a></sup> and molecular evidence supports a divergence of the <a href="/wiki/Monk_seal" title="Monk seal">Monachinae</a> (monk seals) and Phocinae lineages 22 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnason2006_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arnason2006-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fossil evidence indicates the sea otter (<i>Enhydra</i>) lineage became isolated in the North Pacific approximately two mya, giving rise to the now-extinct †<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Enhydra_macrodonta&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Enhydra macrodonta (page does not exist)">Enhydra macrodonta</a></i> and the modern sea otter, <i>Enhydra lutris</i>. The sea otter evolved initially in northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hokkaid%C5%8D" title="Hokkaidō">Hokkaidō</a> and Russia, and then spread east to the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a>, mainland <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, and down the North American coast. In comparison to cetaceans, sirenians, and pinnipeds, which entered the water approximately 50, 40, and 20 mya, respectively, the sea otter is a relative newcomer to marine life. In some respects though, the sea otter is more fully adapted to water than pinnipeds, which must haul out on land or ice to give birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fulcrum_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-fulcrum-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polar bears are thought to have diverged from a population of <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">brown bears</a>, <i>Ursus arctos</i>, that became isolated during a period of glaciation in the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeMaster1981_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeMaster1981-25">[25]</a></sup> or from the eastern part of <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamchatka" title="Kamchatka">Kamchatka</a> and the Kolym Peninsula).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Refere_improving_foraging_efficiencynceC_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Refere_improving_foraging_efficiencynceC-26">[26]</a></sup> The oldest known polar bear fossil is a 130,000 to 110,000-year-old jaw bone, found on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prince_Charles_Foreland" title="Prince Charles Foreland">Prince Charles Foreland</a> in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindqvist_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lindqvist-27">[27]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> (mtDNA) of the polar bear diverged from the brown bear roughly 150,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindqvist_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lindqvist-27">[27]</a></sup> Further, some <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a> of brown bear, as assessed by their mtDNA, are more closely related to polar bears than to other brown bears,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waits_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waits-28">[28]</a></sup> meaning that the polar bear might not be considered a species under some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species_concepts" title="Species concepts">species concepts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marine_mammal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Predicted_patterns_of_marine_mammal_species_richness.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2673" data-file-width="1641" height="358" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Predicted_patterns_of_marine_mammal_species_richness.png/220px-Predicted_patterns_of_marine_mammal_species_richness.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Predicted_patterns_of_marine_mammal_species_richness.png/330px-Predicted_patterns_of_marine_mammal_species_richness.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Predicted_patterns_of_marine_mammal_species_richness.png/440px-Predicted_patterns_of_marine_mammal_species_richness.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Predicted_patterns_of_marine_mammal_species_richness.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Marine mammal species richness: A) All species (n = 115), B) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odontocetes" title="Odontocetes">Odontocetes</a> (n = 69), C) <a href="/wiki/Baleen_whale" title="Baleen whale">Mysticetes</a> (n = 14), D) <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">Pinnipeds</a> (n = 32), based on data from 1990–1999<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Marine mammals are widely distributed throughout the globe, but their distribution is patchy and coincides with the productivity of the oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berta_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-berta-31">[31]</a></sup> Species richness peaks at around 40° latitude, both north and south. This corresponds to the highest levels of <a href="/wiki/Primary_production" title="Primary production">primary production</a> around North and South <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>. Total species range is highly variable for marine mammal species. On average most marine mammals have ranges which are equivalent or smaller than one-fifth of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ship_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-ship-8">[8]</a></sup> The variation observed in range size is a result of the different ecological requirements of each species and their ability to cope with a broad range of environmental conditions. The high degree of overlap between marine mammal species richness and areas of <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human impact on the environment</a> is of concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kasc_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-kasc-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most marine mammals, such as seals and sea otters, inhabit the coast. Seals, however, also use a number of terrestrial habitats, both continental and island. In temperate and tropical areas, they <a href="/wiki/Hauling-out" title="Hauling-out">haul-out</a> on to sandy and <a href="/wiki/Pebble" title="Pebble">pebble</a> beaches, <a href="/wiki/Rocky_shore" title="Rocky shore">rocky shores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shoal" title="Shoal">shoals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mud_flat" title="Mud flat">mud flats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tide_pool" title="Tide pool">tide pools</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Sea_cave" title="Sea cave">sea caves</a>. Some species also rest on man-made structures, like <a href="/wiki/Pier" title="Pier">piers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jetty" title="Jetty">jetties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buoy" title="Buoy">buoys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">oil platforms</a>. Seals may move further inland and rest in sand dunes or vegetation, and may even climb cliffs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thepinnipeds_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-thepinnipeds-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:96</sup> Most cetaceans live in the open ocean, and species like the <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">sperm whale</a> may dive to depths of −1,000 to −2,500 feet (−300 to −760 m) in search of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Sirenians live in shallow coastal waters, usually living 30 feet (9.1 m) below sea level. However, they have been known to dive to −120 feet (−37 m) to forage deep-water <a href="/wiki/Seagrass" title="Seagrass">seagrasses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Sea otters live in protected areas, such as rocky shores, <a href="/wiki/Kelp_forest" title="Kelp forest">kelp forests</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">barrier reefs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> although they may reside among <a href="/wiki/Drift_ice" title="Drift ice">drift ice</a> or in sandy, muddy, or silty areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many marine mammals seasonally migrate. Annual ice contains areas of water that appear and disappear throughout the year as the weather changes, and seals migrate in response to these changes. In turn, polar bears must follow their prey. In <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Bay" title="James Bay">James Bay</a>, and some other areas, the ice melts completely each summer (an event often referred to as "ice-floe breakup"), forcing polar bears to go onto land and wait through the months until the next freeze-up. In the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Sea" title="Chukchi Sea">Chukchi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_Sea" title="Beaufort Sea">Beaufort</a> seas, polar bears retreat each summer to the ice further north that remains frozen year-round.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-distribution_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-distribution-37">[37]</a></sup> Seals may also migrate to other environmental changes, such as <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a>, and traveling seals may use various features of their environment to reach their destination including geomagnetic fields, water and wind currents, the position of the sun and moon and the taste and temperature of the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thepinnipeds_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-thepinnipeds-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:256–257</sup> Baleen whales famously migrate very long distances into tropical waters to give birth and raise young,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> possibly to prevent predation by killer whales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrin2009.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3D2rkHQpToi9sC.26pg.3DPA360_p._360.5D_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrin2009.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3D2rkHQpToi9sC.26pg.3DPA360_p._360.5D-39">[39]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gray_whale" title="Gray whale">gray whale</a> has the longest recorded migration of any mammal, with one traveling 14,000 miles (23,000 km) from the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Okhotsk" title="Sea of Okhotsk">Sea of Okhotsk</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baja_Peninsula" title="Baja Peninsula">Baja Peninsula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> During the winter, manatees living at the northern end of their range migrate to warmer waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptations">Adaptations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marine_mammal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Adaptations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_locomotion" title="Aquatic locomotion">Aquatic locomotion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:482px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dolphin_Anatomy.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1493" data-file-width="2493" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dolphin_Anatomy.svg/480px-Dolphin_Anatomy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dolphin_Anatomy.svg/720px-Dolphin_Anatomy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dolphin_Anatomy.svg/960px-Dolphin_Anatomy.svg.png 2x" width="480"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dolphin_Anatomy.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The anatomy of a <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphin</a> showing its skeleton, major organs, and body shape</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Marine mammals have a number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiological" title="Physiological">physiological</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomical" title="Anatomical">anatomical</a> features to overcome the unique challenges associated with aquatic living. Some of these features are very species specific. Marine mammals have developed a number of features for efficient <a href="/wiki/Animal_locomotion" title="Animal locomotion">locomotion</a> such as torpedo shaped bodies to reduce drag; modified limbs for propulsion and <a href="/wiki/Steering" title="Steering">steering</a>; tail flukes and <a href="/wiki/Dorsal_fin" title="Dorsal fin">dorsal fins</a> for propulsion and balance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berta_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-berta-31">[31]</a></sup> Marine mammals are adept at <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">thermoregulation</a> using dense <a href="/wiki/Fur" title="Fur">fur</a> or <a href="/wiki/Blubber" title="Blubber">blubber</a>, circulatory adjustments (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counter-current_heat_exchange" title="Counter-current heat exchange">counter-current heat exchangers</a>); torpedo shaped bodies, reduced appendages, and large size to prevent heat loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berta_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-berta-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Marine mammals are able to dive for long periods of time. Both pinnipeds and cetaceans have large and complex <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessel</a> systems which serve to store <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to support deep diving. Other important reservoirs include <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">spleen</a> which all have the capacity to hold a high concentration of oxygen. They are also capable of <a href="/wiki/Bradycardia" title="Bradycardia">bradycardia</a> (reduced heart rate), and <a href="/wiki/Vasoconstriction" title="Vasoconstriction">vasoconstriction</a> (shunting most of the oxygen to vital organs such as the brain and heart) to allow extended diving times and cope with oxygen deprivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berta_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-berta-31">[31]</a></sup> If oxygen is depleted, marine mammals can access substantial reservoirs of <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a> that support <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_glycolysis" title="Anaerobic glycolysis">anaerobic glycolysis</a> of the cells involved during conditions of systemic <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_in_fish" title="Hypoxia in fish">hypoxia</a> associated with prolonged submersion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HochachkaStorey1975_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-HochachkaStorey1975-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sound travels differently through water, and therefore marine mammals have developed adaptations to ensure effective communication, prey capture, and predator detection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-white_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-white-45">[45]</a></sup> The most notable adaptation is the development of <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocation</a> in whales and dolphins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berta_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-berta-31">[31]</a></sup> Toothed whales emit a focused beam of high-frequency clicks in the direction that their head is pointing. Sounds are generated by passing air from the bony nares through the phonic lips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cranford_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-cranford-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span class="url"><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=SJ7eBwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA112" rel="nofollow">p. 112</a></span></sup> These sounds are reflected by the dense concave bone of the cranium and an air sac at its base. The focused beam is modulated by a large fatty organ known as the 'melon'. This acts like an acoustic lens because it is composed of lipids of differing densities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cranford_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-cranford-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:121</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Marine mammals have evolved a wide variety of features for feeding, which are mainly seen in their dentition. For example, the cheek teeth of pinnipeds and odontocetes are specifically adapted to capture fish and squid. In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Baleen_whale" title="Baleen whale">baleen whales</a> have evolved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baleen_plate" title="Baleen plate">baleen plates</a> to filter feed <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a> and small fish from the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berta_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-berta-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polar bears, otters, and <a href="/wiki/Fur_seal" title="Fur seal">fur seals</a> have fur, one of the defining mammalian features, that is long, oily, and waterproof in order to trap air to provide insulation. In contrast, other marine mammals – such as whales, dolphins, porpoises, manatees, dugongs, and walruses – have lost long fur in favor of a thick, dense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidermis_(skin)" title="Epidermis (skin)">epidermis</a> and a thickened fat layer (blubber) in response to hydrodynamic requirements. Wading and bottom-feeding animals (such as manatees) need to be heavier than water in order to keep contact with the floor or to stay submerged. Surface-living animals (such as sea otters) need the opposite, and free-swimming animals living in open waters (such as dolphins) need to be neutrally buoyant in order to be able to swim up and down the water column. Typically, thick and dense bone is found in bottom feeders and low bone density is associated with mammals living in deep water. Some marine mammals, such as polar bears and otters, have retained four weight-bearing limbs and can walk on land like fully terrestrial animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reidenberg_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reidenberg-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marine_mammal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marine_mammal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg"><img alt="A killer whale sticking the front end of its body out of the water onto a floating piece of sea-ice to grab a dark-brown seal with light-brown-spots" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="962" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg/220px-Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg/330px-Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg/440px-Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Killer_whale" title="Killer whale">Killer whale</a> hunting a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weddel_seal" title="Weddel seal">Weddel seal</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All cetaceans are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnivorous" title="Carnivorous">carnivorous</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predatory" title="Predatory">predatory</a>. Toothed whales mostly feed on fish and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalopods" title="Cephalopods">cephalopods</a>, followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustaceans" title="Crustaceans">crustaceans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalves" title="Bivalves">bivalves</a>. Some may forage with other kinds of animals, such as other species of whales or certain species of pinnipeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thepinnipeds_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-thepinnipeds-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:169</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> One common feeding method is herding, where a pod squeezes a school of fish into a small volume, known as a <a href="/wiki/Bait_ball" title="Bait ball">bait ball</a>. Individual members then take turns plowing through the ball, feeding on the stunned fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrin2009pp._570.E2.80.93572_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrin2009pp._570.E2.80.93572-50">[50]</a></sup> Coralling is a method where dolphins chase fish into shallow water to catch them more easily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrin2009pp._570.E2.80.93572_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrin2009pp._570.E2.80.93572-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Killer_whale" title="Killer whale">Killer whales</a> and bottlenose dolphins have also been known to drive their prey onto a beach to feed on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Other whales with a blunt snout and reduced dentition rely on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suction_feeding" title="Suction feeding">suction feeding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Though carnivorous, they house <a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">gut flora</a> similar to that of terrestrial herbivores, probably a <a href="/wiki/Vestigiality" title="Vestigiality">remnant</a> of their herbivorous ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Baleen whales use their baleen plates to sieve plankton, among others, out of the water; there are two types of methods: lunge-feeding and gulp-feeding. Lunge-feeders expand the volume of their jaw to a volume bigger than the original volume of the whale itself by inflating their mouth. This causes grooves on their throat to expand, increasing the amount of water the mouth can store.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> They ram a baitball at high speeds in order to feed, but this is only energy-effective when used against a large baitball.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Gulp-feeders swim with an open mouth, filling it with water and prey. Prey must occur in sufficient numbers to trigger the whale's interest, be within a certain size range so that the baleen plates can filter it, and be slow enough so that it cannot escape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrin2009pp._806.E2.80.93813_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrin2009pp._806.E2.80.93813-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sea_otter_with_sea_urchin.jpg"><img alt="A sea urchin floating on the water on its back holding a sea urchin with one hand and a rock in the other" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1650" data-file-width="1650" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sea_otter_with_sea_urchin.jpg/220px-Sea_otter_with_sea_urchin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sea_otter_with_sea_urchin.jpg/330px-Sea_otter_with_sea_urchin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sea_otter_with_sea_urchin.jpg/440px-Sea_otter_with_sea_urchin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sea_otter_with_sea_urchin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sea_otter" title="Sea otter">Sea otters</a> have dexterous hands which they use to smash sea urchins off rocks.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Otters are the only marine animals that are capable of lifting and turning over rocks, which they often do with their front paws when searching for prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-wp-59">[59]</a></sup> The sea otter may pluck <a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snails</a> and other organisms from kelp and dig deep into underwater mud for <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">clams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-wp-59">[59]</a></sup> It is the only marine mammal that catches fish with its forepaws rather than with its teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nickerson21_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-nickerson21-60">[60]</a></sup> Under each foreleg, sea otters have a loose pouch of skin that extends across the chest which they use to store collected food to bring to the surface. This pouch also holds a rock that is used to break open shellfish and clams, an example of <a href="/wiki/Tool_use_by_sea_otters" title="Tool use by sea otters">tool use</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haley_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-haley-61">[61]</a></sup> The sea otters eat while floating on their backs, using their forepaws to tear food apart and bring to their mouths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanblaricom_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-vanblaricom-63">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marine_otter" title="Marine otter">Marine otters</a> mainly feed on crustaceans and fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pinnipeds mostly feed on fish and <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a>, followed by crustaceans and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a>, and then <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and warm-blooded prey (like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_bird" title="Sea bird">sea birds</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thepinnipeds_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-thepinnipeds-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:145</sup> Most species are <a href="/wiki/Generalist_and_specialist_species" title="Generalist and specialist species">generalist</a> feeders, but a few are specialists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> They typically hunt non-schooling fish, slow-moving or immobile invertebrates or endothermic prey when in groups. Solitary foraging species usually exploit coastal waters, bays and rivers. When large schools of fish or squid are available, pinnipeds <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_hunting" title="Cooperative hunting">hunt cooperatively</a> in large groups, locating and herding their prey. Some species, such as <a href="/wiki/California_sea_lion" title="California sea lion">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_American_sea_lion" title="South American sea lion">South American</a> sea lions, may forage with cetaceans and sea birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thepinnipeds_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-thepinnipeds-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:168</sup></p>
<p>The polar bear is the most carnivorous species of bear, and its diet primarily consists of <a href="/wiki/Ringed_seal" title="Ringed seal">ringed</a> (<i>Pusa hispida</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Bearded_seal" title="Bearded seal">bearded</a> (<i>Erignathus barbatus</i>) seals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Polar bears hunt primarily at the interface between ice, water, and air; they only rarely catch seals on land or in open water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The polar bear's most common hunting method is still-hunting:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hemstock24-27_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-hemstock24-27-68">[68]</a></sup> The bear locates a seal breathing hole using its sense of smell, and crouches nearby for a seal to appear. When the seal exhales, the bear smells its breath, reaches into the hole with a forepaw, and drags it out onto the ice. The polar bear also hunts by stalking seals resting on the ice. Upon spotting a seal, it walks to within 100 yards (90 m), and then crouches. If the seal does not notice, the bear creeps to within 30 to 40 feet (9 to 10 m) of the seal and then suddenly rushes to attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> A third hunting method is to raid the birth lairs that female seals create in the snow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hemstock24-27_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-hemstock24-27-68">[68]</a></sup> They may also feed on fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dugong_Marsa_Alam.jpg"><img alt="A dugong with its mouth on the sandy seafloor, leaving a noticeable cloud which hovers near the bottom. There are two yellow fish with black stripes near its mouth, and there are grasses poking out of the seafloor" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Dugong_Marsa_Alam.jpg/220px-Dugong_Marsa_Alam.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Dugong_Marsa_Alam.jpg/330px-Dugong_Marsa_Alam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Dugong_Marsa_Alam.jpg/440px-Dugong_Marsa_Alam.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dugong_Marsa_Alam.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Dugong" title="Dugong">dugong</a> feeding on the sea-floor</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sirenians are referred to as "sea cows" because their diet consists mainly of sea-grass. When eating, they ingest the whole plant, including the roots, although when this is impossible they feed on just the leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> A wide variety of seagrass has been found in dugong stomach contents, and evidence exists they will eat <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> when seagrass is scarce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> West Indian manatees eat up to 60 different species of plants, as well as fish and small invertebrates to a lesser extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Keystone_species">Keystone species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marine_mammal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Keystone species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">Keystone species</a></div>
<p>Sea otters are a classic example of a keystone species; their presence affects the ecosystem more profoundly than their size and numbers would suggest. They keep the population of certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benthic" title="Benthic">benthic</a> (sea floor) herbivores, particularly <a href="/wiki/Sea_urchin" title="Sea urchin">sea urchins</a>, in check. Sea urchins graze on the lower stems of <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a>, causing the kelp to drift away and die. Loss of the habitat and nutrients provided by kelp forests leads to profound <a href="/wiki/Cascade_effect_(ecology)" title="Cascade effect (ecology)">cascade effects</a> on the marine ecosystem. North Pacific areas that do not have sea otters often turn into <a href="/wiki/Urchin_barren" title="Urchin barren">urchin barrens</a>, with abundant sea urchins and no kelp forest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Reintroduction of sea otters to British Columbia has led to a dramatic improvement in the health of coastal ecosystems,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dfo_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-dfo-75">[75]</a></sup> and similar changes have been observed as sea otter populations recovered in the Aleutian and Commander Islands and the <a href="/wiki/Big_Sur" title="Big Sur">Big Sur</a> coast of California<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanblaricom_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-vanblaricom-63">[63]</a></sup> However, some kelp forest <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> in California have also thrived without sea otters, with sea urchin populations apparently controlled by other factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanblaricom_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-vanblaricom-63">[63]</a></sup> The role of sea otters in maintaining kelp forests has been observed to be more important in areas of open coast than in more protected bays and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estuaries" title="Estuaries">estuaries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanblaricom_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-vanblaricom-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:358px;max-width:358px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">

</div>

<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blanchon-idlm2006.jpg"><img alt="A white seal pup on the snowy ground with large black eyes and nose" data-file-height="459" data-file-width="523" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Blanchon-idlm2006.jpg/150px-Blanchon-idlm2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Blanchon-idlm2006.jpg/225px-Blanchon-idlm2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Blanchon-idlm2006.jpg/300px-Blanchon-idlm2006.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div>
